【11†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


This theme is important to Bruno, who symptomatically picks it up
again in the poem at the beginning of the Explicatio triginta sigillorum and
“intimates his hope for the advent of a ‘mythical’ age when the world, wise
at last, though late (sero sapiens), will see ignorance and hostile prejudices
removed from its surface.”34 These ideas are rooted in a philosophy of his-
tory inspired by Hermeticism that finds complete expression in the Spaccio,
where Bruno even translates (while modifying deeply) the Lament of
Hermes Trismegistus. In other words, these ideas are central in Bruno’s

“…per speculum et in aenigmate…”

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 49

50 Epistemic Practices of a Revolutionary

mind and, although they emerge for the first time at this stage of his
research, they are constantly considered right up to his last works, for
example in some remarkable pages of the De immenso.

To investigate the reasons for the recurrence of these issues from both
linguistic and conceptual viewpoints means to face the problem of the
dynamics of Bruno’s ‘mind,’ a task that cannot be accomplished here. In
the context of our line of reasoning, however, some elements can be point-
ed out: First, these subjects, also outlined in De umbris, appear in a text
that, for a long time, has been counted amongst the mnemotechnical works
and implicitly dismissed due to an obsolete idea of Renaissance culture
and philosophy. However, we must drop this misinterpretation if we want
to understand Bruno’s theories, in this particular text and in general.

This text, in fact, has strong philosophical caliber and is perhaps one of
the most relevant in Bruno’s œuvre. Yet, due to its composite structure, it
also has the important merit of taking us straight into Bruno’s ‘workshop,’
allowing us to both understand the manner in which his texts must be deci-
phered in general and to define (or confirm) at least four specific points.
These include the articulated theoretical basis of his ars, which tackles a
number of gnosiologial, ontological, and ethical issues, evenly introducing
them into the frame of the nova filosofia; the fact that ethico-political and
religious issues are already established in his mnemotechnical works and
destined to find a fuller development in the Spaccio (where the contro -
versy against Puritans becomes central as much as vehement); the possi-
bility, as a consequence, of dating the genesis of Bruno’s first conception
of the Spaccio to the last period of his stay in Paris, though in the frame of
a polemic that, in the Sigillus, is aimed against Protestants as well as
against monastic orders; and, last but not least, the text allows us to figure
out Bruno’s modus operandi.

Yet, one needs not exaggerate the relation between the Sigillus and the
Spaccio. The persistency of these topics, even if in a short time span,
attests to the continuity of some main themes and also to the incessant
revision to which Bruno subjected his works.

【12†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


Sergius Kodera also examines Candelaio in his essay. He uncovers a
line of political thought, an implicit commentary on important historical,
political affairs, running throughout the whole comedy, leading up to
Machiavelli. Although Bruno never even mentions the name of the philo -
sopher-politician from Florence, Kodera detects the subterranean pre sence
of Machiavelli in strategically important passages of Candelaio. He shows

Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 7

8 Turning Traditions Upside Down

how Bruno adapts the Machiavellian notions of vicissitudo and occasione
and parallels Bruno’s re-evaluation of the sense of touch with Machi a -
velli’s ideas: For both authors, it is hands-on knowledge rather than de -
tached vision that is conducive to true insight and acquisition of power.
Whereas Bruno makes it clear that this re-evaluation involves heavy meta-
physical implications, Machiavelli and Bruno do share common ground
again in their vehement criticism of Christianity as harmful to the commu-
nity.

This section concludes with Henning Hufnagel’s analysis of Bruno’s
shortest dialogue, Cabala del cavallo pegaseo. Hufnagel focuses on the dia-
logue’s twisted structure. Cabala is one of the most disputed of Bruno’s
texts and scholars have made many attempts to fix a positive theoretical
me aning in the dialogue. Hufnagel shows that this is a hopeless fight
because Bruno blends the dialogue with forms of paradoxical epideixis:
Bruno piles up paradoxes until all claims negate and cancel each other.
Hufnagel proposes Cabala should instead be read as a satire of knowledge.
The transmission of alleged knowledge between the interlocutors collapses
continually and Bruno lays bare their modes of argumentation. If anything
is to be learned from Cabala, it is how the literary form of the dialogue can
acquire the value of an argument. Thus, Cabala can be understood as a
reflection on the dialectical possibilities of the dialogue as a genre.

In the fourth section in this volume, Reflections of an Intellectual Burning:
Bruno’s Reception and Literary Afterlife, the horizon widens both themat-
ically and temporally. This section looks at some of the traces Bruno’s life
and work left in other philosophical, or even theological contexts, presents
aspects of his sometimes curious literary afterlife—which extends to
Bertolt Brecht and beyond—and tries to come to terms with the truly last-
ing fascination exerted by the man from Nola.

Wilhelm Schmidt-Biggemann explores the possibilities of picturing me -
ta physics by analysing a copper engraving in Heinrich Khunrath’s Amphi -
theatrum Sapientiae Aeternae. In this image, the Kabbalistic foundation of
all being, both the divine and the creatural, is symbolically depicted. For
Bruno, the externalization of the first cause—which is in itself unknow-
able, because it only reveals itself in its expression—is, at the same time,
the beginning of all spatiality. This idea corresponds materially to the
imaging of the first cause, as it is represented in the Leipzig theosopher
Khunrath’s great work Amphitheatrum Sapientiae Aeternae. He goes beyond
Bruno, however, when he problematizes the question of the symbolism of
the divine externalizations.

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 8

【13†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


5

by transforming traditions, he expounds a theory of a heroic philosophical
life. The Platonic allegory of the soul living in a cave of intellectual blind-
ness serves as a central symbol demonstrating the ignorance and philo-
sophical narrowness of traditional as well as contemporary thought, which
avoids looking at the truth through the “eyes of reason.” Following the
antique model, Bruno takes the interconnection of natural philosophy,
epistemology and moral philosophy as a starting point to criticize a false
and delimited cosmology, to establish the theory of the infinite universe
containing innumerable worlds and to reject a short-sighted interpretation
of sensory perceptions as a basis for epistemic evidence. Referring to the
philosophy of Epicurus and his poetical follower Lucretius, combined with
neo-Platonic motifs of the soul’s ascent to the One, Bruno breaks through
the limited caves of the physical and epistemological as well as the misin-
terpreted moral world. The self-awareness of the true philosophical exis-
tence, engaged in an infinite process of venture and vicissitude, opens the
eyes to the manifestation of the One, which is the deepest ground inside
the soul.

The second section of this volume is entitled Experience and Vision of a
New Cosmic Order: Giordano Bruno’s Natural Philosophy. Here, central
aspects of Bruno’s cosmology and his transformation of classical concepts
of natural philosophy are explored. It focuses on problems of cosmic order
and the conceptualization of infinity in a physical, mathematical, and
metaphysical sense. Re-formulating traditional concepts of space and loca-
tion, extensive and intensive infinity, motion and time, Bruno confronts us
with a comprehensive transformation of philosophical and scientific
thought.

Commenting the third chapter of De immenso et innumerabilibus,
Miguel A. Granada elucidates how Bruno, after having presented the the-
ological and anthropological impact of cosmic infinity, conceptualizes the
structure of the infinite universe, embracing innumerable planetary sys-
tems. Referring to the Epicurean concept of a homogenous infinite uni-
verse, Bruno argues that in this infinite cosmic space there is no centre or
periphery but only a relativity of positions. Due to this Epicurean homo-
geneity, there must be a similarity of structures in the whole universe. And
for that reason, due to a specific point of view, every star can be seen as the
centre of an orbicular system of surrounding planets. As Granada points
out, Bruno is the first philosopher who postulates a plurality of “worlds”
or planetary systems, affirming at the same time that there is one law of
nature regulating all cosmic movements. He, therefore, goes beyond tradi-

Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 5

6 Turning Traditions Upside Down

tional restrictions—which are still binding Kepler, e.g.—and, in doing so,
anticipates the cosmological reforms of the seventeenth-century as repre-
sented by Huygens or Newton.

7

no’s position within the history of natural sciences. He presents him as a
serious contemporary of Galilei. Although there are deep philosophical
and methodological differences, both agree that Copernicus’s heliocentric
system is not just a mathematical hypothesis but an explanation of the real
cosmic structures. Whereas Bruno’s convictions are based on the study of
ancient philosophy, the basis of Galilei’s theories are innovative instru-
mental practices that allow him to make precise observations and calcula-
tions. Both share an anti-Aristotelian impetus, abolishing the distinction
between supralunar and sublunar physics: Both attest there is but one
homogeneous world, governed by one natural law. Finally, they analogo -
usly interpret the Scriptures regarding natural knowledge. In fact, Bru no
literally anticipates the allegoric interpretation of the Scriptures later
adopted by Galilei: The aim of the Bible and of religious dogma in gener-
al is social and moral discipline and not the description of natural truth.

The third section of this volume, Forms of Non-Conformity: Bruno’s Works
as Literary Texts, shifts the focus from Bruno’s ideas to his texts. Bruno
was a versatile, highly experimental writer, well-experienced in many gen-
res and styles and familiar with numerous, especially heterodox and eccen-
tric traditions. His disposition for polemics, satire and paradox, his lin-
guistic inventiveness and scorn of literary decorum make his texts
colourful and compelling reading. The essays presented here take a look at
the literary aspect of Bruno’s philosophy and examine how the form of
Bruno’s texts determines their meaning.

Nuccio Ordine takes on the tripartite structure of Bruno’s comedy
Candelaio, and asks why not only one, as in other plays, but three stock
characters of Renaissance comedy are ridiculed here. He argues that the
answer is to be found in the theory of the comic as set forth in Plato’s
Philebus. Here, Socrates identifies three basic situations of ridicule—situ-
ations perfectly embodied by the three protagonists of Candelaio. Thus,
Ordine sheds light on the way Bruno works when writing his texts. Stres -
sing the gnoseological function of the comic in Bruno’s text, he finally indi-
cates some central elements of Bruno’s philosophy.

Sergius Kodera also examines Candelaio in his essay. He uncovers a
line of political thought, an implicit commentary on important historical,
political affairs, running throughout the whole comedy, leading up to
Machiavelli. Although Bruno never even mentions the name of the philo -
sopher-politician from Florence, Kodera detects the subterranean pre sence
of Machiavelli in strategically important passages of Candelaio. He shows

how Bruno adapts the Machiavellian notions of vicissitudo and occasione
and parallels Bruno’s re-evaluation of the sense of touch with Machi a -

tached vision that is conducive to true insight and acquisition of power.
Whereas Bruno makes it clear that this re-evaluation involves heavy meta-
physical implications, Machiavelli and Bruno do share common ground
again in their vehement criticism of Christianity as harmful to the commu-

【16†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


Paul Richard Blum opens the volume with an epistemological reflection
on default values, in reference to a passage from Bruno’s Latin poem De
immenso. While default settings are constitutive instruments of every cog-
nitive process—they function as a sort of practical, temporary prejudice—
Blum accentuates Bruno’s request for an epistemic meta-operation. In his
poem, Bruno reminds us to be conscious of the tentativeness of mental de -
fault values in the same way as children are aware of the fact that changing
sights of one and the same object are related to their point of view. If the
in tellect fails to realize the infinity of the universe, it is not the fault of the
visual sense, but of the unconsidered reliance on vision as an epistemic de -
fault value. Bruno advocates a change in the standard principles of thought.

Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 3

4 Turning Traditions Upside Down

This mental operation leads to facing up to a radically altered view of the
world, resulting in the discovery of the philosophy of the infinite.

Covering Bruno’s so-called Frankfurt Trilogy, Angelika Bönker-Vallon
analyses Bruno’s intention to demonstrate the systematic interrelation
bet ween metaphysical, mathematical and physical thought. In these three
Latin poems, Bruno delineates a universal concept of science which
embraces all branches of scientific knowledge. Within this tripartite ency-
clopaedia, De triplici minimo et mensura is of the utmost significance, as
Bönker-Vallon points out, because in this work Bruno tries to conceptual-
ize a mathematical theory for measuring the infinite cosmos or, in a com-
prehensive sense, to create a theoretical substructure of the encyclopaedic
sciences. Based on the concept of a threefold minimum—i.e., God as the
monad of monads, the mathematical point and the physical atom—Bruno
demonstrates the metaphysical implications and qualities of point and
atom as primary functional minima, redefined as first and elementary spa-
tial structures comprising the coincidental relationship of maximum and
minimum, essential for conceiving the relationship between the infinite
space and its fundamental entities. The interconnection of metaphysical,
mathematical and physical minima provides accessibility to the systematic
nature of Bruno’s threefold theory of the infinite, which based on the idea
of a universal measure, allows for the development of the idea of univer-
sal science.

【17†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


To rescue the mnemotechnical texts from oblivion, scholars had to
eliminate the interpretations that dominated the nineteenth-century (and
first half of the twentieth) as they were mostly concerned with identifying
Bruno as a forerunner of various modern schools of thought. To reaffirm
the value and centrality of the mnemotechnical works, it was important to
get rid of a stereotyped image of the Renaissance, of outdated views con-
cerning the relationship between the Renaissance and modernity, and—in
primis—of a unilateral and static view of modernity. This task was neither
simple nor short; however, it is thanks to the pioneering investigations car-
ried out in the course of the twentieth-century by scholars like Warburg,
Garin, Rossi, Vasoli, and Frances A. Yates that magic and mnemotechnics
are now commonly perceived as central features of the Renaissance med-
itation on mankind, nature, and experience. The constitutive core of
Giordano Bruno’s thought would be impossible to decipher without taking
his mnemotechnical works into consideration.

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 36

37

Bruno’s idea of the impossibility of a process of thinking which is not
“thinking through images” is the operating and unfolding “principle” in
his mnemotechnical works, making them the most fascinating, ingenious
and arduous text of the nova filosofia. It is a sort of Urtext, essential to the
understanding of the genesis, features, and development of his philosophy.
Studying his experiments with mnemotechnics means gaining direct access
to Bruno’s “philosophical workshop” and access to this workshop is an
indispensable tool for comprehending Bruno’s “mind” and for following
the patterns from which the key elements of the nova filosofia matured.
These texts are very complex, sometimes obscure, and difficult to inter-
pret. Still, how they were reduced to mere extravagant oddities that placed
Bruno in the category of “pre-modernity” is one of those mysteries that
only historians are able to deconstruct. This is not surprising; after all,
there is no historiography without categories or, indeed, without ideolo-
gies. It would be enough to keep in mind (as a preventive therapy) that the
Past is another Present, and should be studied accordingly.3

In a passage from a recent commentary to Explicatio triginta sigillorum,
there is a statement, which can be espoused in every respect:

The brain thinks through images, because all abstract and conceptu-
al information originates from the reading and interpretation of fan-
tastic figures—either natural or artificial—and the fantastic dimen-
sion acts as interface between the logic dimension and Nature, since
all the experience of the world is seen through it, as in a mirror; it is
not, however, a passive and uncontrolled magma: in fact, since it
“bor ders” on the cogitative faculty, it is constantly open to the ratio-
nal dimension, which incessantly derives from and pours into the
partial and progressive impressions that are the object of its elabo-
rations. In conclusion, mnemonic loci and the images of memories,
ideated by Bruno, are but peculiar and defined modalities – regulat-
ed by the laws and principles of a specific ars—of a cognitive opera-
tive principle spontaneous and innate in man which also receives a
sort of enhancement and optimization when rationality ‘descends
upon’ it to regulate its actions.4

Give me a close reading of the discussion of Yates

Assistant

Here’s a detailed analysis of the discussion of Frances Yates in the book "Turning Traditions Upside Down" regarding her interpretation of Giordano Bruno:

Context of Yates's Influence


  • Frances Yates is acknowledged for her pivotal role in bringing Bruno’s connection to Hermeticism to the forefront of modern scholarship.

  • In her work, particularly Giordano Bruno and the Hermetic Tradition (1964), Yates portrays Bruno as a mystical thinker deeply rooted in the Hermetic tradition, emphasizing his role as a magus rather than a philosopher or scientist.


Critique of Yates in the Volume


  • Reduction to Hermeticism: The volume critiques Yates for overly simplifying Bruno’s intellectual profile by centering Hermeticism as the dominant framework for his thought. The editors argue that while Hermeticism was significant, Bruno’s synthesis of ideas is broader, incorporating Neo-Platonism, Copernican cosmology, and Aristotelian critique alongside Hermetic elements.

  • Misrepresentation of Modernity: Yates is seen as positioning Bruno as a figure looking backward to mystical traditions rather than forward to modern philosophy and science. In contrast, this volume argues that Bruno's Hermeticism was a vehicle for his forward-looking vision of an infinite and dynamic universe.

  • Philosophical Depth vs. Mysticism: The book contends that Yates underappreciates the philosophical rigor of Bruno’s work, presenting Hermeticism primarily as a mystical, magical tradition. The authors highlight how Bruno reinterpreted Hermetic ideas in service of a systematic cosmology and epistemology.


Key Differences in Interpretation


  • Focus on Hermetic Texts: Yates emphasizes Bruno’s use of texts like the Corpus Hermeticum to develop a magical cosmology. This volume points out that Bruno’s engagement with Hermeticism is not merely mystical but philosophical, using its symbolic language to explore concepts like infinity, unity, and cosmic order.

  • Integration of Traditions: Yates’s reading isolates Hermeticism as a standalone influence. The volume argues for a more integrated perspective, where Hermeticism interacts with Neo-Platonism, Epicurean physics, and Renaissance natural philosophy to inform Bruno’s ideas.

  • Modern Scientific Relevance: While Yates casts Bruno as outside the trajectory of modern science, this volume positions him as a proto-modern thinker, with Hermeticism providing a metaphorical framework rather than a mystical doctrine.


Support from the Text


The discussion explicitly situates Yates within the historiography of Bruno scholarship, acknowledging her contributions while critiquing her tendency to overstate Hermeticism's dominance. Several essays in the volume address this tension, particularly in the context of Bruno’s Spaccio de la Bestia Trionfante and De immenso et innumerabilibus, where Hermeticism is interwoven with broader metaphysical and cosmological arguments【22:3†source】【22:11†source】.

Takeaway


The book respects Yates's influence but seeks to revise her interpretation, presenting Bruno as a thinker who transcends Hermeticism to engage in a more universal intellectual project. This nuanced view highlights Bruno’s role in the transition from Renaissance traditions to early modern philosophy.

Let me know if you’d like further details or specific quotes!

Historiography of Bruno's LegacyExplores the evolving interpretations of Bruno in scholarship, including mystical, philosophical, and modern scientific perspectives.Illuminates how different intellectual traditions (e.g., Hermeticism, Neo-Platonism) shaped the historiographical image of Bruno.Highlights the tension between viewing Bruno as a mystical Renaissance figure and as a proto-modern philosopher/scientist.Bruno's legacy is a complex interplay of historical reinterpretations, reflecting shifting academic and cultural priorities."The fascination with Bruno has evolved in tandem with historiographical shifts, from mysticism to modernity."Historical discussions in the book, including critiques of Frances Yates and references to modern scholarship on Bruno’s cosmology and natural philosophy.

This table highlights historiographical concepts relating to Bruno's intellectual legacy, focusing on the interplay of interpretations by scholars like Frances Yates and the more nuanced perspective offered in "Turning Traditions Upside Down." Let me know if you'd like specific sections expanded!

Here’s a broader table of historiographical concepts derived from the main thesis of Turning Traditions Upside Down, highlighting how it critiques, reframes, and builds on historical perspectives about Bruno’s legacy.

SectionContentsContributionsChallengesArgumentQuotationSources
Reconstructing Bruno's LegacyFocuses on Bruno as a revolutionary thinker reshaping epistemology, metaphysics, and cosmology in the context of Renaissance intellectual traditions.Proposes a multidimensional view of Bruno, integrating Hermeticism, Neo-Platonism, and emerging scientific thought into his philosophical framework.Challenges monolithic portrayals of Bruno, such as those seeing him solely as a martyr or as a mystical figure, by presenting his systematic and rational contributions.Bruno’s works deconstruct and rearrange traditional systems, offering a new vision of knowledge and the cosmos rooted in philosophical and scientific innovation."Bruno’s philosophy represents a turning point between traditional and modern intellectual paradigms."The book’s introduction and essays emphasizing Bruno’s synthesis of traditions and his transformative role in cosmology.
Deconstruction of TraditionsHighlights Bruno’s critique of Aristotelian and Ptolemaic paradigms, focusing on his methods of deconstruction and reconfiguration of classical traditions.Frames Bruno as a disruptor of established epistemic and metaphysical norms, particularly through his critique of geocentrism and finite cosmologies.Risks positioning Bruno’s deconstruction as wholly oppositional rather than as part of a dialectical engagement with inherited traditions.Bruno’s critique of tradition is not simply a rejection but a reconstruction, integrating elements of older systems into a radically new framework."To understand Bruno is to see how he reconstructs what he deconstructs, offering a new synthesis of old traditions."Essays on epistemology and cosmology in Turning Traditions Upside Down, particularly the sections addressing Bruno’s critique of Scholasticism.
Cosmological ModernityExamines Bruno’s concept of an infinite, centerless universe and its implications for the scientific and philosophical thought of his time and beyond.Demonstrates Bruno’s contribution to modern cosmology by rejecting anthropocentric and hierarchical models of the universe.Engages with debates on whether Bruno’s cosmology was scientific or philosophical, and whether it prefigures or diverges from modern physics.Bruno’s cosmology bridges Renaissance metaphysics and modern scientific thought, positioning him as a precursor to the infinite universe models in science."Bruno conceives a universe without center or periphery, where all worlds are of equal importance."References to Bruno’s De immenso et innumerabilibus and comparisons to Copernican and Newtonian ideas throughout the volume.
Philosophy and MnemotechnicsInvestigates Bruno’s use of memory systems as epistemological tools, blending Hermetic and Renaissance techniques into his metaphysical explorations.Expands on how Bruno’s use of mnemonics reflects a sophisticated epistemology that connects human cognition to the structure of the universe.Risks overemphasizing the symbolic and mystical aspects of mnemonics while underappreciating their practical philosophical function.Mnemotechnics in Bruno’s thought serves as a bridge between mental operations and the infinite structure of the cosmos, aligning human cognition with universal principles."The art of memory is for Bruno not mere technique, but a cosmological tool."Discussions of De umbris idearum and Explicatio triginta sigillorum in essays on epistemology and methodology in the book.
Reception and TransformationExplores how Bruno’s works have been received, interpreted, and transformed in different intellectual and cultural contexts from the Renaissance to modernity.Highlights the diverse ways Bruno has been appropriated: as a mystical figure, a proto-modern scientist, and a champion of intellectual freedom.Critiques overly narrow or ideological appropriations of Bruno, such as those focusing exclusively on his martyrdom or mystical associations.Bruno’s intellectual legacy is dynamic, reflecting the changing priorities and debates of each era, from the Counter-Reformation to 20th-century scholarship."Bruno has been continuously reinterpreted, his ideas reflecting the priorities of different intellectual epochs."Essays on Bruno’s reception, including his influence on Jesuit scholars, 20th-century modernists, and the Warburg Institute’s research.
Renaissance as a Transitional EraPositions Bruno’s thought as emblematic of the Renaissance as a bridge between medieval scholasticism and modern philosophy and science.Demonstrates how Bruno integrates Renaissance humanism, Hermeticism, and Neo-Platonism into a framework that anticipates modern scientific and philosophical developments.Challenges traditional historiographical boundaries that treat the Renaissance as distinct from modernity, emphasizing continuities instead.Bruno exemplifies the Renaissance’s role in reshaping intellectual traditions, merging classical influences with forward-looking innovations in cosmology and epistemology."Bruno embodies the Renaissance spirit, transforming the past to envision the future."Essays on Bruno’s cosmology, epistemology, and the Renaissance intellectual landscape in the book.

SectionContentsContributionsChallengesArgumentQuotationSources
The Metaphysical OneCentral Neo-Platonic concept emphasizing the unity of all being, where the One is the source of all reality and transcends all opposites and distinctions.Influences Bruno’s idea of an infinite, indivisible cosmos where all distinctions dissolve into metaphysical unity.Reconciling the abstract, ineffable nature of the One with its practical implications for cosmology and human understanding.Bruno adopts the One as a foundation for his metaphysical and cosmological systems, integrating it with Renaissance ideas about infinity and nature."The One is the origin of all realities and all possibilities, preceding even the Aristotelian principles of form and matter."Plotinus’s Enneads and Neo-Platonic interpretations by Ficino and Renaissance philosophers in Bruno’s works like De immenso.
Unity of Being and ThoughtThe Neo-Platonic analogy between mind and world, where the structure of thought reflects the universal order of being.Provides Bruno with a framework to link individual cognition to the infinite structure of the cosmos.Integrating this analogy into a dynamic cosmology that rejects static Aristotelian hierarchies.Bruno’s philosophy emphasizes that the human mind can approximate universal truths through iterative thought processes, reflecting cosmic unity."For Bruno, the unity of mind and cosmos is a foundational principle that bridges epistemology and metaphysics."Neo-Platonic ideas reinterpreted in Bruno’s epistemological works, including De umbris idearum.
Emanation and ReturnThe process of creation as an emanation from the One, where all things ultimately strive to return to their source.Shapes Bruno’s ethical and cosmological frameworks, emphasizing a dynamic universe in constant motion toward unity.Adapting the static hierarchy of Neo-Platonic emanation to Bruno’s vision of an infinite, centerless, and dynamic cosmos.Bruno reimagines the universe as a space of infinite emanation and interaction, rejecting fixed hierarchies in favor of dynamic relational systems."All things emanate from and return to the One, in an infinite interplay of cosmic forces."Bruno’s reinterpretation of Plotinus and Proclus in cosmological works such as De immenso and De triplici minimo.
Cosmic Order and InfinityNeo-Platonic cosmology emphasizes the ordered structure of the universe, with all levels of being reflecting the harmony of the One.Inspires Bruno’s infinite cosmology, which extends the idea of harmony to an infinite, centerless cosmos with no privileged perspectives.Reconciling traditional Neo-Platonic order with the infinite, non-hierarchical universe Bruno proposes.Bruno expands the Neo-Platonic idea of cosmic harmony into a framework that accommodates an infinite universe with innumerable worlds."Infinity is the ultimate expression of cosmic order, extending the harmony of the One to boundless possibilities."References to Ficino and Plotinus in Bruno’s infinite universe model in De immenso.
Ascent of the SoulThe soul’s journey from ignorance and material attachment to union with the One through intellectual and spiritual purification.Influences Bruno’s ethical framework, advocating for a "heroic life" where individuals strive for unity with metaphysical truths.Translating the Neo-Platonic ascent into a practical ethical philosophy that aligns with Bruno’s vision of infinite exploration and human potential.Bruno reframes the ascent of the soul as an intellectual and ethical endeavor, aligning it with his vision of heroic philosophical inquiry."The heroic life is an endless journey of striving for unity with the infinite truths of the One."Ethical discussions in Bruno’s dialogues and philosophical writings, influenced by Plotinus and Ficino.
Matter and Spirit as UnityNeo-Platonism views matter and spirit as different manifestations of the same underlying reality, united by the One.Shapes Bruno’s rejection of dualistic systems, emphasizing the immanence of the divine in all aspects of nature.Overcoming the static categorizations of matter and spirit in traditional Neo-Platonic frameworks to support Bruno’s dynamic cosmological vision.Bruno’s cosmology integrates matter and spirit into a unified, living universe, reflecting the immanence of divine principles in all things."Matter and spirit are not opposites but two aspects of the same infinite unity."Neo-Platonic influences in Bruno’s metaphysics, particularly in De la causa, principio et uno.

This table captures the core Neo-Platonic ideas and their adaptation in Bruno’s philosophy, emphasizing their contributions to his metaphysical, cosmological, and ethical frameworks. Let me know if you’d like specific examples expanded!

User

make a table of scholastic ideas

Assistant

SectionContentsContributionsChallengesArgumentQuotationSources
Aristotelian Substance TheoryScholasticism’s reliance on Aristotelian metaphysics, where reality is understood in terms of substances and accidents.Provides a framework for analyzing the composition of the natural world and the interactions of matter and form.Bruno critiques the static nature of substance theory, proposing instead a dynamic unity where matter and form are inseparable and interconnected.Scholastic ideas of substance and accident are too rigid to accommodate Bruno’s vision of a living, infinite cosmos."Substance, as defined in Aristotelian terms, cannot capture the fluid and dynamic nature of reality."Aristotelian philosophy and its Scholastic interpretations in Bruno’s critiques, particularly in De la causa, principio et uno.
Hierarchical CosmosScholastic cosmology upholds a finite, hierarchical universe with Earth at the center, based on Aristotelian-Ptolemaic models.Reinforces the medieval worldview and the coherence of the geocentric system.Bruno rejects the geocentric, finite cosmos, advocating for an infinite, centerless universe governed by universal laws rather than divine hierarchy.Scholastic cosmology is incompatible with Bruno’s infinite universe, which decentralizes humanity and Earth."The universe has no center, no periphery, and no fixed hierarchy of being."Ptolemaic-Aristotelian influences evident in the cosmological debates of the Renaissance and Bruno’s responses in De immenso et innumerabilibus.
Faith and ReasonScholasticism emphasizes the harmony between faith and reason, where philosophical inquiry serves to clarify and support religious doctrine.Establishes a structured approach to reconciling theological and philosophical principles.Bruno challenges the dominance of theological doctrine over free inquiry, advocating for the independence of philosophy from religious constraints.Faith and reason should operate independently, as Bruno argues for a philosophy unrestricted by religious dogma."Philosophy should not serve theology but should seek truth on its own terms."Discussions in Spaccio de la Bestia Trionfante and De la causa, principio et uno, where Bruno critiques the subjugation of philosophy to theology.
Causality and TeleologyScholasticism emphasizes final causes, where natural phenomena are explained in terms of their ultimate purpose within a divine plan.Provides a teleological framework for understanding natural and metaphysical processes.Bruno rejects final causes as limiting, proposing instead a naturalistic explanation of phenomena based on intrinsic principles and infinite motion.Teleological explanations are inadequate for Bruno’s vision of a universe governed by intrinsic, dynamic principles rather than predetermined purposes."Nature’s movements are infinite, without end or preordained purpose."Aristotelian teleology adapted by Scholastic thinkers, critiqued in Bruno’s works such as De immenso et innumerabilibus.
Analogy of Being (Analogia Entis)Scholasticism’s principle that being exists analogically across different levels, reflecting divine order.Reinforces the hierarchical structure of the cosmos, linking all levels of existence through a divine order.Bruno reinterprets analogy to emphasize unity over hierarchy, rejecting the strict levels of being in favor of a dynamic continuum of existence.Analogy should reflect the unity and interconnectedness of all things rather than reinforce rigid hierarchical divisions."Being is not divided into discrete hierarchies but flows continuously across all existence."Scholastic interpretations of analogy critiqued and transformed in Bruno’s metaphysical works, especially De la causa, principio et uno.
Prime MoverScholastic thought incorporates Aristotle’s concept of the Prime Mover, a divine force responsible for the motion and order of the cosmos.Aligns natural motion with divine intention, creating a coherent cosmological framework tied to theological principles.Bruno critiques the necessity of a Prime Mover, proposing instead a self-sustaining, infinite universe governed by immanent principles of motion and change.The infinite universe operates without the need for a Prime Mover, as motion is intrinsic to nature itself."The universe moves by its own principles, not by the dictates of a distant Prime Mover."Aristotelian and Scholastic cosmology critiqued in Bruno’s De immenso et innumerabilibus and De la causa, principio et uno.

This table outlines key Scholastic ideas, their role in Bruno’s intellectual environment, and how he challenges and reinterprets them to develop his own revolutionary philosophy. Let me know if you’d like more detail or specific sections expanded!

make a table of biographical details from bruno's life

Assistant

Life StageDetailsSignificanceChallenges FacedTakeaway
Early Life (1548–1562)Born in Nola, Kingdom of Naples. Baptized as Filippo Bruno; later took the name Giordano upon entering religious life.Grew up in a period of intense religious and intellectual transformation in Renaissance Europe.Exposure to dogmatic religious teachings, limiting intellectual freedom.Bruno’s early years were shaped by the intellectual and cultural environment of Southern Italy, influencing his later critiques of orthodoxy.
Dominican Order (1562–1576)Entered the monastery of San Domenico Maggiore in Naples; studied theology, philosophy, and Aristotelianism.Gained formal education in Scholasticism, which he later critiqued, and was exposed to Neo-Platonic and Hermetic texts.Accused of heresy for unorthodox ideas, including rejecting the veneration of images and questioning core Catholic doctrines.His monastic education provided him with a deep foundation in the intellectual traditions he later deconstructed.
Exile and Wanderings (1576–1583)Fled the Dominican Order to escape charges of heresy. Traveled through Italy, France, Switzerland, and England, seeking intellectual freedom.Encountered diverse intellectual currents, including Copernicanism and Protestant Reformation ideas, which shaped his cosmological views.Constant threats of persecution, poverty, and lack of stable patronage during his travels.Bruno’s exile fueled his intellectual development, exposing him to new ideas while reinforcing his identity as a wandering philosopher.
Paris and London (1583–1585)Lectured at the University of Paris and enjoyed patronage from King Henry III. Moved to London, writing works like The Ash Wednesday Supper.Developed his ideas on an infinite universe, plurality of worlds, and critiques of Aristotelian cosmology.Criticism and hostility from academics and clergy, especially in England, where his ideas were considered radical.Bruno’s stay in these intellectual centers allowed him to articulate and publish many of his revolutionary cosmological and philosophical ideas.
Germany and Prague (1586–1590)Taught in Wittenberg and Helmstedt; briefly lived in Prague under Emperor Rudolf II’s patronage.Continued writing key works, including De triplici minimo and De immenso, blending Hermeticism, mathematics, and cosmology.Limited financial support and growing suspicion from Protestant reformers, who also found his ideas controversial.Despite challenges, Bruno maintained his prolific output, expanding his cosmological theories and philosophical frameworks.
Return to Italy (1591–1592)Invited to Venice by nobleman Giovanni Mocenigo, who initially supported Bruno but later denounced him to the Inquisition.Attempted to find patronage in his homeland, showing his continued desire for intellectual recognition.Betrayed by Mocenigo, leading to his arrest and the beginning of his trial by the Inquisition.Bruno’s return to Italy marked the final phase of his life, illustrating the risks he took in seeking acceptance for his ideas.
Trial and Execution (1592–1600)Tried by the Roman Inquisition for heresy, including charges of denying the Trinity, advocating for the plurality of worlds, and pantheism.Became a symbol of intellectual freedom and defiance against religious orthodoxy after refusing to recant his beliefs.Endured seven years of imprisonment and interrogation before being burned at the stake in Campo de' Fiori, Rome, on February 17, 1600.Bruno’s martyrdom highlights the conflict between dogma and intellectual exploration during the Counter-Reformation.

This table provides a chronological overview of key events in Bruno’s life, emphasizing their significance, challenges, and enduring legacy. Let me know if you’d like to explore specific periods in more detail!

Here is a timeline of Giordano Bruno’s intellectual career:

Year(s)EventSignificanceKey Works or Contributions
1562Entered the Dominican Order in Naples.Began formal studies in theology, philosophy, and Aristotelianism, and encountered Neo-Platonic and Hermetic texts.Foundations in Scholasticism and exposure to Renaissance humanism and alternative traditions like Hermeticism.
1576Fled the monastery after accusations of heresy.Marked the beginning of Bruno’s wandering intellectual career and rejection of religious orthodoxy.Departure from orthodox theology; embraced intellectual independence.
1579–1581Studied and taught in Geneva, Toulouse, and Paris.Engaged with Protestant Reformation ideas and began articulating his critiques of Aristotelian cosmology and theology.Early lectures critiquing dogmatic approaches to philosophy and theology.
1583–1585Lived in London under the patronage of the French ambassador.Published key philosophical and cosmological works, integrating Copernicanism with his vision of an infinite, centerless universe.The Ash Wednesday Supper (Cena de le Ceneri), The Expulsion of the Triumphant Beast (Spaccio de la Bestia Trionfante).
1586–1588Taught in Wittenberg and Helmstedt.Further developed his ideas on infinity, the unity of matter, and the nature of the universe, blending Hermeticism with scientific thought.De triplici minimo et mensura, De monade, numero et figura.
1589–1590Moved to Prague under Emperor Rudolf II’s patronage.Produced significant works on cosmology and metaphysics, showcasing his mature ideas on infinity and unity.De immenso et innumerabilibus, De rerum principiis.
1591Invited to Venice by Giovanni Mocenigo.Attempted to find support for his ideas in Italy but faced betrayal and arrest.Limited contributions during this period due to political and personal challenges.
1592–1600Imprisoned and tried by the Inquisition in Venice and Rome.Defended his philosophical and cosmological ideas during prolonged trials, refusing to recant.Trial transcripts reveal his steadfast commitment to intellectual freedom.
February 17, 1600Executed by burning at the stake in Campo de' Fiori, Rome.Bruno became a symbol of intellectual resistance to dogma and a martyr for freedom of thought.Legacy as a pioneer of free thought and a precursor to modern cosmological theories.

This timeline highlights the major phases of Bruno’s intellectual development, showing how his ideas evolved and were shaped by his encounters with different intellectual traditions and challenges. Let me know if you'd like more details about specific periods!

Time PeriodEvent or ActionTrouble FacedCauseOutcome
1562–1576Entered the Dominican Order and began studying theology and philosophy.Accused of heresy for possessing prohibited texts, questioning the veneration of saints, and doubting Catholic doctrines.Early challenges to orthodoxy, including his rejection of central Catholic beliefs such as the Trinity.Fled the Dominican monastery in 1576 to avoid trial by the Inquisition.
1576–1579Wandered through Italy, avoiding the Inquisition.Fear of arrest and charges of heresy pursued him across Italian cities.His reputation as a dissident theologian and intellectual put him at constant risk.Left Italy and moved to Geneva.
1579Moved to Geneva, a Calvinist stronghold, seeking refuge.Excommunicated by Calvinist authorities for criticizing a professor and publishing a pamphlet without permission.His outspoken critique of dogmatic figures clashed with the strict Protestant environment.Forced to leave Geneva and moved to Toulouse.
1583–1585Lived in London, supported by the French ambassador.Faced hostility from English academics and clergy for his unorthodox ideas about religion and philosophy, as well as his confrontational tone.His Cena de le Ceneri ridiculed Aristotelian cosmology and attacked academic orthodoxy, offending many intellectuals.Left England and moved to Germany to seek a more tolerant intellectual environment.
1586Taught in Helmstedt, a Protestant region.Excommunicated by the Lutheran Church for "blasphemy" and unorthodox teachings.Continued to critique religious dogma and challenge institutional authority, regardless of denomination.Left Helmstedt and moved to Prague.
1591Returned to Italy under the invitation of Giovanni Mocenigo in Venice.Arrested and denounced to the Inquisition by Mocenigo, who accused him of heresy, blasphemy, and improper religious beliefs.Mocenigo felt betrayed when Bruno refused to teach him mystical and magical practices.Imprisoned and interrogated by the Venetian Inquisition before being transferred to Rome.
1592–1600Imprisoned in Rome by the Roman Inquisition.Charged with heresy for denying the Trinity, rejecting transubstantiation, advocating the plurality of worlds, and claiming pantheistic beliefs.Bruno’s refusal to recant his philosophical and cosmological ideas led to prolonged imprisonment and interrogation.Executed by burning at the stake in Campo de' Fiori, Rome, on February 17, 1600.

This table details Bruno's confrontations with religious and academic authorities at each stage of his life, illustrating how his unorthodox ideas consistently brought him into conflict with prevailing institutions. Let me know if you'd like more detail on any particular episode!

mankind. This leads us to the reflection and the reception of Bruno’s intel-
lectual fire. Bruno has been received in literature and art as well as in pub-
lic spaces in a way that has been as versatile as the philosopher himself.
The sculpture created by Berlin artist Alexander Polzin, entitled Giordano
Bruno, is the subject of the final chapter of this volume. This artwork was
originally commissioned by the Central European University in Budapest
to celebrate George Soros’s seventieth birthday and copies were erected in
Budapest and Nola. On the occasion of our colloquium, the statue was also
inaugurated at Potsdamer Platz in the center of Berlin. The statue depicts
the vulnerable body of a philosopher who is turning the world upside-down
and attempting to dislodge the borders of the universe—a fitting symbol of
Bruno’s tragedy, a man who dared to reconsider the fundamentals of the
world as they had been passed down throughout the ages and who struggled
to find his place in Europe. Yet, after peregrination across the con tin ent, he
ended up cast as a heretic in all three Christian churches of the occident.
He is now honored in a place, at the centre of modern Eu rope, which also
once served as the stage for the tragedy of a world divided.

We would like to conclude by thanking all the authors who contributed
to this volume. Most of all, we would like to thank the editors, Henning
Hufnagel and Anne Eusterschulte, for their substantial contributions and
the great work they have achieved. We hope that this volume will help cre-
ate a better understanding of a philosopher whose thinking is more preva-
lent today than any of his contemporaries might have foreseen.

Berlin and Budapest, September 2012
Jürgen Renn

Director of the Max Planck Institute
for the History of Science, Berlin

Yehuda Elkana (1934–2012)

Introduction

Turning traditions upside down—this metaphor seems to be a good start-
ing point for rethinking Giordano Bruno’s philosophy and his provocative
aspirations in the world of letters. It certainly would have been to Bruno’s
liking; someone who, in his writings, often presented himself as a Mercu ri -
an, larger-than-life figure who has come to put things straight: the No lan,
enlightening his contemporaries about the misleading paths of traditional
doctrines and opening minds for the discovery of true philosophy.

Preface

Giordano Bruno: A non-conformist at the dawn of an epoch, a martyr of
modernity, or just a polemic controvert? Descriptions of this kind fall
short of grasping the multifold character of the Nolan. Deconstructing the
relationship between God and the outside world gave distinction to his life
—and his refusal to recant when facing the stake somehow set him apart
from the world. Since then, the debate about the Nolan has been domi-
nated by the interest in the comprehensive œuvre of the philosopher, and
by attempts to make him a protagonist of the freedom of thought.

This volume brings together some of the most eminent researchers to
have worked on Giordano Bruno and his epoch. Their contributions—
inspired by a colloquium on Giordano Bruno organized by the Max Planck
Institute for the History of Science and the Central European University
in 2008—represent a critical inventory of our knowledge of the Nolan’s life
and work as well as shedding light on the many dimensions of Bruno’s
thinking. His radical rationality, starting with attempts to reinvent the
roots of religion but ending with a break in the doctrines of trinity and
monotheism; his natural philosophy, including the cosmology of the infini-
ty of the universe and of the unlimited number of worlds, or his thinking
on the vital character of all the objects on earth; his vision of a new cosmic
order and his place in the Copernican revolution, breaking with the domi-
nant heliocentric and anthropocentric system in a way more radical than
any contemporary thinker had before; his scientific methods, his analogies,
speculations, metaphors, his intuitive as well as practical knowledge,
reflecting the epistemic practices of a revolutionary; his idiosyncratic
blending of traditions, both philosophical and literary; his linguistic exu-
berance and conceptual boldness; the textual structures, genres, and forms
of non-conformity Bruno continuously experimented with, from the trac-
tate to comedy, from dialogue to poetry; and finally, his gusto for paradox
and satirical panache. Attention has also to be given to Bruno’s consider-
ation of the role of philosophy in society—either as a way to give coher-
ence to a world thrown off track, or simply as an instrument for civilizing

De immenso et innumerabilibus, book I, chapter 3.1

Published in Frankfurt in 1591, Giordano Bruno’s De immenso et innu-
merabilibus is the last and the most mature and complete exposition of his
cosmological ideas. However, the significance of this work does not con-
cern only the cosmological and physical domain, since it contains also a
lucid presentation of Bruno’s vindication of philosophy as the proper path
to human perfection and intellectual happiness through the scientific

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:08 Oldal 91

【3†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


3

In order to bring into focus the horizon and the dimensions of Bruno’s
transformation of traditions, to demonstrate how he turned contemporary
thinking upside down, and to delineate his persuasive strategies, as well as
the theoretical consequences of his thought, the contributions of this vol-
ume all start from a selected text, an exemplary passage drawn from Bru -
no’s great range of work or from texts close to his person and œuvre. Pre -
senting and elucidating pivotal points of the philosophia nolana, the essays
both invite readers to become acquainted with Bruno’s philosophy and
provide them with innovative dissections, interpretations and conceptual
reflections that will prepare the way for a revised reading of Bruno’s
works. They reconstruct Bruno’s deconstruction and rearrangement of
phi lo sophical systems, his understanding of nature and the cosmos, his
methods of thinking and writing, his aesthetic and literary categories. They
ex plore the topics and modi operandi of the great Renaissance thinker and
point out the variance of his reception up to the present, both inside and
outside academia, exhibiting the ever-changing relevance and continuing
fascination of Bruno’s work.

The results of the contributor’s investigations are presented in four sec-
tions, according to four main fields of research interest. The first section
entitled Epistemic Practices of a Revolutionary: Bruno’s Methods and Thin -
king, examines Bruno’s methodological procedures. The essays show how
the Nolan undermined traditional positions, by a subtle reflexion on epis-
temological principles, logical structures, or metaphysical presuppositions,
to secure evidence for his philosophy of the infinite and its inherent con-
sequences which revolutionized the fields of metaphysics and epistemolo-
gy, physics, cosmology and anthropology. Bruno’s method of appropriat-
ing and concatenating older philosophical concepts appears to be both a
method of critical revision as well as systematic transformation.

Paul Richard Blum opens the volume with an epistemological reflection
on default values, in reference to a passage from Bruno’s Latin poem De
immenso. While default settings are constitutive instruments of every cog-
nitive process—they function as a sort of practical, temporary prejudice—
Blum accentuates Bruno’s request for an epistemic meta-operation. In his
poem, Bruno reminds us to be conscious of the tentativeness of mental de -
fault values in the same way as children are aware of the fact that changing
sights of one and the same object are related to their point of view. If the
in tellect fails to realize the infinity of the universe, it is not the fault of the
visual sense, but of the unconsidered reliance on vision as an epistemic de -
fault value. Bruno advocates a change in the standard principles of thought.

This mental operation leads to facing up to a radically altered view of the
world, resulting in the discovery of the philosophy of the infinite.

viii Turning Traditions Upside Down

mankind. This leads us to the reflection and the reception of Bruno’s intel-
lectual fire. Bruno has been received in literature and art as well as in pub-
lic spaces in a way that has been as versatile as the philosopher himself.
The sculpture created by Berlin artist Alexander Polzin, entitled Giordano
Bruno, is the subject of the final chapter of this volume. This artwork was
originally commissioned by the Central European University in Budapest
to celebrate George Soros’s seventieth birthday and copies were erected in
Budapest and Nola. On the occasion of our colloquium, the statue was also
inaugurated at Potsdamer Platz in the center of Berlin. The statue depicts
the vulnerable body of a philosopher who is turning the world upside-down
and attempting to dislodge the borders of the universe—a fitting symbol of
Bruno’s tragedy, a man who dared to reconsider the fundamentals of the
world as they had been passed down throughout the ages and who struggled
to find his place in Europe. Yet, after peregrination across the con tin ent, he
ended up cast as a heretic in all three Christian churches of the occident.
He is now honored in a place, at the centre of modern Eu rope, which also
once served as the stage for the tragedy of a world divided.

the great work they have achieved. We hope that this volume will help cre-

lent today than any of his contemporaries might have foreseen.

【6†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 5

6 Turning Traditions Upside Down

tional restrictions—which are still binding Kepler, e.g.—and, in doing so,
anticipates the cosmological reforms of the seventeenth-century as repre-
sented by Huygens or Newton.

Wolfgang Neuser presents an alternative interpretation of Bruno’s the-
ory of matter. Against the backdrop of the controversial medieval debates
about atomism concerning the attributes or qualities of atoms, their cor-
puscular or essential substantiality, and the appreciation of them as dis-
tinct particles, Bruno develops an understanding of matter that merges the
Aristotelian concept of substance with atomistic ideas and the Neo-Pla -
tonic concept of matter, according to which matter is filled with spirit.
Bruno differentiates three manifestations of the metaphysical monad, i.e.,
the undivided, infinite unum that systematically underlies everything.
These manifestations are the constituents of matter: spiritus, based on the
neo-Platonic concept of a spiritual virtue inherent in all matter; spatium,
or the structural explication of the indivisible, un-extended point, which
emerges in a process of self-generation; and atomus, the smallest indivisi-
ble or the physical minimum. Spirit, space, and atom are the structural
modes of the expansion of infinite unity. Based on this conceptualisation
of matter, Neuser characterizes Bruno as a Renaissance philosopher who,
at the end of the post-traditional stage in history, anticipates the subject-
based foundation of knowledge, representative for thinkers from the Mo -
dern Age like Descartes, Spinoza or Leibniz.

Enrico Giannetto’s chapter emphasizes no less than the astonishing fact
that Giordano Bruno—notwithstanding the significant differences in theo-
retical premises—presents a theory of relativity of time and motion three
hundred years before Poincaré and Einstein. While his non-mechanistic
conception of nature was an obstacle in recognizing Bruno as a serious sci-
entist, according to Giannetto, Bruno’s importance within the history of
theoretical physics is irrefutable. He argues that the relativity of time is
one consequence of Bruno’s concept of cosmic infinity, also entailing the
relativity of the localisation of planets or stars and of their motion, rota-
tion and orbits within a uniform space. Rejecting the Aristotelian assump-
tion of a primum mobile, Bruno argues for the plurality of incommensu-
rable times due to the infinity of uniform motions within the universe.
Turning the Aristotelian doctrine upside down, Bruno regards (relative)
motion as the measure of time.

Arcangelo Rossi, comparing the cosmological theories of Copernicus,
Bruno, and Galilei, attests a striking correspondence between the Nolan
and the Galilean doctrines. Like Giannetto, Rossi aims at restoring Bru -

pretation of the statue at the moment of its unveiling at Potsdamer Platz. He
construes the heretic as an emblematic figure of a young, independent
Europe and asks himself why Berlin—a city which Bruno, this great tra -
veller, has never been to—and, specifically, an underground station could be

the right place to commemorate the philosopher from Nola. For Grün bein,

Anything but virtual were the exchanges we—philosophers, historians, and

Introduction

Turning traditions upside down—this metaphor seems to be a good start-
ing point for rethinking Giordano Bruno’s philosophy and his provocative
aspirations in the world of letters. It certainly would have been to Bruno’s
liking; someone who, in his writings, often presented himself as a Mercu ri -
an, larger-than-life figure who has come to put things straight: the No lan,
enlightening his contemporaries about the misleading paths of traditional
doctrines and opening minds for the discovery of true philosophy.

Born in 1548 near the town of Nola in the Kingdom of Naples, Gior -
dano Bruno is known today as an exceptional, yet ambivalent figure with-
in the history of ideas. As a great scholar, celebrated for his comprehen-
sive erudition, famous for his emphatic defence of philosophical inquiry
against religious doctrine and his rigid attacks against reputed authorities,
he caused an intellectual stir wherever he went. Sometimes his interventi -
ons were received with great éclat; more often, however, they created aca -
de mic scandal. Bruno impressed the French King Henri III who made him
one of his “lecteurs royaux”; three years later, he had to leave Paris in
tumultuous circumstances after a disputation had grown out of control. In
the meantime, Bruno had upset the intellectual scene in Elizabethan
England with his six Italian dialogues. Moving between different frontiers,
confessional beliefs, philosophical schools, academic parties and institu-
tional interests, Bruno—who did not mince matters—came under attack
from many sides.

Bruno argued relentlessly for a replacement of the philosophical koiné
of his time, the Scholastic Aristotelian philosophy that presented a view of
man and cosmos, which Bruno had been dissatisfied with since his time in a
Dominican convent in Naples. For his project of philosophical reform, Bruno
draws on a large variety of sources: on Platonic, pre-Socratic, Py tha gorean,
and Hermetic ideas, on thinkers such as Plotinus, Averroes, Nicholas of
Cusa, Raimundus Lullus and Ficino. He builds on the metho do logy, flour-
ishing during the Renaissance, of mnemotechnics. And he uses all these

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 1

2 Turning Traditions Upside Down

【9†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 6

7

no’s position within the history of natural sciences. He presents him as a
serious contemporary of Galilei. Although there are deep philosophical
and methodological differences, both agree that Copernicus’s heliocentric
system is not just a mathematical hypothesis but an explanation of the real
cosmic structures. Whereas Bruno’s convictions are based on the study of
ancient philosophy, the basis of Galilei’s theories are innovative instru-
mental practices that allow him to make precise observations and calcula-
tions. Both share an anti-Aristotelian impetus, abolishing the distinction
between supralunar and sublunar physics: Both attest there is but one
homogeneous world, governed by one natural law. Finally, they analogo -
usly interpret the Scriptures regarding natural knowledge. In fact, Bru no
literally anticipates the allegoric interpretation of the Scriptures later
adopted by Galilei: The aim of the Bible and of religious dogma in gener-
al is social and moral discipline and not the description of natural truth.

The third section of this volume, Forms of Non-Conformity: Bruno’s Works
as Literary Texts, shifts the focus from Bruno’s ideas to his texts. Bruno
was a versatile, highly experimental writer, well-experienced in many gen-
res and styles and familiar with numerous, especially heterodox and eccen-
tric traditions. His disposition for polemics, satire and paradox, his lin-
guistic inventiveness and scorn of literary decorum make his texts
colourful and compelling reading. The essays presented here take a look at
the literary aspect of Bruno’s philosophy and examine how the form of
Bruno’s texts determines their meaning.

Nuccio Ordine takes on the tripartite structure of Bruno’s comedy
Candelaio, and asks why not only one, as in other plays, but three stock
characters of Renaissance comedy are ridiculed here. He argues that the
answer is to be found in the theory of the comic as set forth in Plato’s
Philebus. Here, Socrates identifies three basic situations of ridicule—situ-
ations perfectly embodied by the three protagonists of Candelaio. Thus,
Ordine sheds light on the way Bruno works when writing his texts. Stres -
sing the gnoseological function of the comic in Bruno’s text, he finally indi-
cates some central elements of Bruno’s philosophy.

Sergius Kodera also examines Candelaio in his essay. He uncovers a
line of political thought, an implicit commentary on important historical,
political affairs, running throughout the whole comedy, leading up to
Machiavelli. Although Bruno never even mentions the name of the philo -
sopher-politician from Florence, Kodera detects the subterranean pre sence
of Machiavelli in strategically important passages of Candelaio. He shows

Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 7

8 Turning Traditions Upside Down

how Bruno adapts the Machiavellian notions of vicissitudo and occasione
and parallels Bruno’s re-evaluation of the sense of touch with Machi a -
velli’s ideas: For both authors, it is hands-on knowledge rather than de -
tached vision that is conducive to true insight and acquisition of power.
Whereas Bruno makes it clear that this re-evaluation involves heavy meta-
physical implications, Machiavelli and Bruno do share common ground
again in their vehement criticism of Christianity as harmful to the commu-
nity.

【10†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


Paul Richard Blum opens the volume with an epistemological reflection
on default values, in reference to a passage from Bruno’s Latin poem De
immenso. While default settings are constitutive instruments of every cog-
nitive process—they function as a sort of practical, temporary prejudice—
Blum accentuates Bruno’s request for an epistemic meta-operation. In his
poem, Bruno reminds us to be conscious of the tentativeness of mental de -
fault values in the same way as children are aware of the fact that changing
sights of one and the same object are related to their point of view. If the
in tellect fails to realize the infinity of the universe, it is not the fault of the
visual sense, but of the unconsidered reliance on vision as an epistemic de -
fault value. Bruno advocates a change in the standard principles of thought.

Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 3

4 Turning Traditions Upside Down

This mental operation leads to facing up to a radically altered view of the
world, resulting in the discovery of the philosophy of the infinite.

Covering Bruno’s so-called Frankfurt Trilogy, Angelika Bönker-Vallon
analyses Bruno’s intention to demonstrate the systematic interrelation
bet ween metaphysical, mathematical and physical thought. In these three
Latin poems, Bruno delineates a universal concept of science which
embraces all branches of scientific knowledge. Within this tripartite ency-
clopaedia, De triplici minimo et mensura is of the utmost significance, as
Bönker-Vallon points out, because in this work Bruno tries to conceptual-
ize a mathematical theory for measuring the infinite cosmos or, in a com-
prehensive sense, to create a theoretical substructure of the encyclopaedic
sciences. Based on the concept of a threefold minimum—i.e., God as the
monad of monads, the mathematical point and the physical atom—Bruno
demonstrates the metaphysical implications and qualities of point and
atom as primary functional minima, redefined as first and elementary spa-
tial structures comprising the coincidental relationship of maximum and
minimum, essential for conceiving the relationship between the infinite
space and its fundamental entities. The interconnection of metaphysical,
mathematical and physical minima provides accessibility to the systematic
nature of Bruno’s threefold theory of the infinite, which based on the idea
of a universal measure, allows for the development of the idea of univer-
sal science.

Giordano Bruno: A non-conformist at the dawn of an epoch, a martyr of modernity, or just a
polemic controvert? Descriptions of this kind fall short of grasping the multifold character of “the
Nolan.” Deconstructing the relationship between God and the outside world gave distinction to
his life—and his refusal to recant when facing the stake somehow set him apart from the world.
Since then, the debate about the Nolan has been dominated by the interest in the comprehensive
oeuvre of the philosopher, and by attempts to make him a protagonist of the freedom of thought.

This volume brings together some of the most eminent researchers to have worked on Giordano
Bruno and his epoch. In order to bring into focus the horizon and the dimensions of his
transformation of traditions, to demonstrate how he turned contemporary thinking upside down,
and to delineate his persuasive strategies, as well as the theoretical consequences of his thought,
the contributions all start from an exemplary passage drawn from Bruno’s great range of work
or from texts close to his person and oeuvre. Presenting and elucidating pivotal points of the
philosophia nolana, the essays both invite readers to become acquainted with Bruno’s philosophy
and provide them with innovative dissections, interpretations and conceptual reflections that
will prepare the way for a revised reading of Bruno’s works. They reconstruct his deconstruction
and rearrangement of philosophical systems, his understanding of nature and the cosmos, his
methods of thinking and writing, his aesthetic and literary categories. They explore the topics
and modi operandi of the great Renaissance thinker and point out the variance of his reception
up to the present, both inside and outside academia, exhibiting the ever-changing relevance and
continuing fascination of Bruno’s work.

Central European University Press
Budapest – New York

C o v e r d e s i g n b y S e b a s t i a n S t a c h o w s k i

【13†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


Sergius Kodera also examines Candelaio in his essay. He uncovers a
line of political thought, an implicit commentary on important historical,
political affairs, running throughout the whole comedy, leading up to
Machiavelli. Although Bruno never even mentions the name of the philo -
sopher-politician from Florence, Kodera detects the subterranean pre sence
of Machiavelli in strategically important passages of Candelaio. He shows

Introduction

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 7

8 Turning Traditions Upside Down

how Bruno adapts the Machiavellian notions of vicissitudo and occasione
and parallels Bruno’s re-evaluation of the sense of touch with Machi a -
velli’s ideas: For both authors, it is hands-on knowledge rather than de -
tached vision that is conducive to true insight and acquisition of power.
Whereas Bruno makes it clear that this re-evaluation involves heavy meta-
physical implications, Machiavelli and Bruno do share common ground
again in their vehement criticism of Christianity as harmful to the commu-
nity.

This section concludes with Henning Hufnagel’s analysis of Bruno’s
shortest dialogue, Cabala del cavallo pegaseo. Hufnagel focuses on the dia-
logue’s twisted structure. Cabala is one of the most disputed of Bruno’s
texts and scholars have made many attempts to fix a positive theoretical
me aning in the dialogue. Hufnagel shows that this is a hopeless fight
because Bruno blends the dialogue with forms of paradoxical epideixis:
Bruno piles up paradoxes until all claims negate and cancel each other.
Hufnagel proposes Cabala should instead be read as a satire of knowledge.
The transmission of alleged knowledge between the interlocutors collapses
continually and Bruno lays bare their modes of argumentation. If anything
is to be learned from Cabala, it is how the literary form of the dialogue can
acquire the value of an argument. Thus, Cabala can be understood as a
reflection on the dialectical possibilities of the dialogue as a genre.

In the fourth section in this volume, Reflections of an Intellectual Burning:
Bruno’s Reception and Literary Afterlife, the horizon widens both themat-
ically and temporally. This section looks at some of the traces Bruno’s life
and work left in other philosophical, or even theological contexts, presents
aspects of his sometimes curious literary afterlife—which extends to
Bertolt Brecht and beyond—and tries to come to terms with the truly last-
ing fascination exerted by the man from Nola.

Wilhelm Schmidt-Biggemann explores the possibilities of picturing me -
ta physics by analysing a copper engraving in Heinrich Khunrath’s Amphi -
theatrum Sapientiae Aeternae. In this image, the Kabbalistic foundation of
all being, both the divine and the creatural, is symbolically depicted. For
Bruno, the externalization of the first cause—which is in itself unknow-
able, because it only reveals itself in its expression—is, at the same time,
the beginning of all spatiality. This idea corresponds materially to the
imaging of the first cause, as it is represented in the Leipzig theosopher
Khunrath’s great work Amphitheatrum Sapientiae Aeternae. He goes beyond
Bruno, however, when he problematizes the question of the symbolism of
the divine externalizations.

Giordano Bruno: A non-conformist at the dawn of an epoch, a martyr of modernity, or just a
polemic controvert? Descriptions of this kind fall short of grasping the multifold character of “the
Nolan.” Deconstructing the relationship between God and the outside world gave distinction to
his life—and his refusal to recant when facing the stake somehow set him apart from the world.
Since then, the debate about the Nolan has been dominated by the interest in the comprehensive
oeuvre of the philosopher, and by attempts to make him a protagonist of the freedom of thought.

【16†Anne Eusterschulte, Henning Hufnagel, (eds.) - Turning Traditions Upside Down_ Rethinking Giordano Bruno's Enlightenment (2013, Central European University Press) - libgen.li.pdf†file-6Cn12XFPtP7xYX748jWeVM】


Right from the beginning, Bruno was an “academico di nulla aca de mia,”
an “academic of no academy,” as he calls himself in his comedy Candle-
bearer. Soon enough, he became not only institutionally, but literally home-
less, wandering from Italy to France, to England, to Germany and back
again over the Alps, watched by suspicious eyes because of his radical visi -
ons of intellectual reform. At last the prominent “universally learned man
who did not, however, have any religion,” as one contemporary de scribed
him, fell victim to denunciation in Venice and, after a nearly eight-year-
long trial, was condemned by the Roman inquisition to be burnt at the
stake. The verdict was executed on the Campo de’ Fiori in Rome, on Feb -
ruary 17, 1600. The stench of burnt flesh is said to have lingered for days.

The tragedy of Bruno’s life and thought may be seen as a radical sym-
bol of the human struggle for knowledge, rising to the challenge of free-
dom of thought at the risk of losing ground. It is upside down, therefore,
that Alexander Polzin’s sculpture shows Bruno.

HUFNAGEL.nyomdai.imprimált.:... 2.4.2013 14:07 Oldal 2

3

In order to bring into focus the horizon and the dimensions of Bruno’s
transformation of traditions, to demonstrate how he turned contemporary
thinking upside down, and to delineate his persuasive strategies, as well as
the theoretical consequences of his thought, the contributions of this vol-
ume all start from a selected text, an exemplary passage drawn from Bru -
no’s great range of work or from texts close to his person and œuvre. Pre -
senting and elucidating pivotal points of the philosophia nolana, the essays
both invite readers to become acquainted with Bruno’s philosophy and
provide them with innovative dissections, interpretations and conceptual
reflections that will prepare the way for a revised reading of Bruno’s
works. They reconstruct Bruno’s deconstruction and rearrangement of
phi lo sophical systems, his understanding of nature and the cosmos, his
methods of thinking and writing, his aesthetic and literary categories. They
ex plore the topics and modi operandi of the great Renaissance thinker and
point out the variance of his reception up to the present, both inside and
outside academia, exhibiting the ever-changing relevance and continuing
fascination of Bruno’s work.

The results of the contributor’s investigations are presented in four sec-
tions, according to four main fields of research interest. The first section
entitled Epistemic Practices of a Revolutionary: Bruno’s Methods and Thin -
king, examines Bruno’s methodological procedures. The essays show how
the Nolan undermined traditional positions, by a subtle reflexion on epis-
temological principles, logical structures, or metaphysical presuppositions,
to secure evidence for his philosophy of the infinite and its inherent con-
sequences which revolutionized the fields of metaphysics and epistemolo-
gy, physics, cosmology and anthropology. Bruno’s method of appropriat-
ing and concatenating older philosophical concepts appears to be both a
method of critical revision as well as systematic transformation.
